	<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Afrikaans</h1>
	
	<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
		<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>

<p><b>Afrikaans</b> (<span class="nowrap"><small><a href="/wiki/British_English" title="British English">UK</a>: </small><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˌ/: secondary stress follows">ˌ</span><span title="/æ/: &#39;a&#39; in &#39;bad&#39;">æ</span><span title="&#39;f&#39; in &#39;find&#39;">f</span><span title="&#39;r&#39; in &#39;rye&#39;">r</span><span title="/ɪ/: &#39;i&#39; in &#39;kit&#39;">ɪ</span><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="&#39;k&#39; in &#39;kind&#39;">k</span><span title="/ɑː/: &#39;a&#39; in &#39;father&#39;">ɑː</span><span title="&#39;n&#39; in &#39;nigh&#39;">n</span><span title="&#39;s&#39; in &#39;sigh&#39;">s</span></span>, <wbr />-<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="&#39;k&#39; in &#39;kind&#39;">k</span><span title="/ɑː/: &#39;a&#39; in &#39;father&#39;">ɑː</span><span title="&#39;n&#39; in &#39;nigh&#39;">n</span><span title="&#39;z&#39; in &#39;zoom&#39;">z</span></span>/</a></span></span>, <span class="nowrap"><small><a href="/wiki/American_English" title="American English">US</a>: </small><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˌ/: secondary stress follows">ˌ</span><span title="/ɑː/: &#39;a&#39; in &#39;father&#39;">ɑː</span><span title="&#39;f&#39; in &#39;find&#39;">f</span></span>-/</a></span></span>)<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> is a <a href="/wiki/West_Germanic_languages" title="West Germanic languages">West Germanic language</a> spoken in <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South Africa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Namibia" title="Namibia">Namibia</a> and, to a lesser extent, <a href="/wiki/Botswana" title="Botswana">Botswana</a> and <a href="/wiki/Zimbabwe" title="Zimbabwe">Zimbabwe</a>. It evolved from the <a href="/wiki/Dutch_language" title="Dutch language">Dutch vernacular</a><sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Heese1971_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Heese1971-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> of <a href="/wiki/South_Holland" title="South Holland">South Holland</a> (<a href="/wiki/Hollandic_dialect" title="Hollandic dialect">Hollandic dialect</a>)<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Heeringade_Wet2015_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Heeringade_Wet2015-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> spoken by the largely <a href="/wiki/Afrikaners" title="Afrikaners">Dutch settlers</a> (and then by the native <a href="/wiki/Africans" class="mw-redirect" title="Africans">Africans</a> who associated with them) in the south-west of what is now South Africa, where it gradually began to develop distinguishing characteristics in the course of the 18th century.<sup id="cite_ref-coetzee_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-coetzee-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Hence, it is a <a href="/wiki/Daughter_language" title="Daughter language">daughter language</a> of Dutch.
</p><p>Although Afrikaans has adopted words from other languages, including <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Khoisan_languages" title="Khoisan languages">Khoisan languages</a>, an estimated 90 to 95% of the vocabulary of Afrikaans is of Dutch origin.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;n 1&#93;</a></sup> Therefore, <a href="/wiki/Differences_between_Afrikaans_and_Dutch" class="mw-redirect" title="Differences between Afrikaans and Dutch">differences with Dutch</a> often lie in the more <a href="/wiki/Analytic_language" title="Analytic language">analytic</a>-type <a href="/wiki/Morphology_(linguistics)" title="Morphology (linguistics)">morphology</a> and grammar of Afrikaans, and a spelling that expresses Afrikaans pronunciation rather than standard Dutch.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;n 2&#93;</a></sup> There is a large degree of <a href="/wiki/Mutual_intelligibility" title="Mutual intelligibility">mutual intelligibility</a> between the two languages—especially in written form.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;n 3&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>With about 7 million native speakers in South Africa, or 13.5% of the population, it is the third-most-spoken language in the country.<sup id="cite_ref-cib11_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cib11-15">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> Estimates of the total number of Afrikaans speakers range between 15 and 23 million.<sup id="cite_ref-speakers_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-speakers-16">&#91;n 4&#93;</a></sup> It has the widest geographical and racial distribution of all the 11 official <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_South_Africa" title="Languages of South Africa">languages of South Africa</a>, and is widely spoken and understood as a second or third language.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;n 5&#93;</a></sup> It is the majority language of the western half of South Africa—the provinces of the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Cape" title="Northern Cape">Northern Cape</a> and <a href="/wiki/Western_Cape" title="Western Cape">Western Cape</a>—and the first language of 75.8% of <a href="/wiki/Coloured" class="mw-redirect" title="Coloured">Coloured</a> South Africans (4.8 million people), 60.8% of White South Africans (2.7 million); 4.6% of <a href="/wiki/Asian_South_African" class="mw-redirect" title="Asian South African">Asian South Africans</a> (58,000 people), and 1.5% of Black South Africans (600,000 people).<sup id="cite_ref-superweb_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-superweb-18">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Afrikaans&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The term is ultimately derived from the Dutch term <i><i lang="nl" title="Dutch language text">Afrikaans-Hollands</i></i> meaning "African Dutch".<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> It was previously referred to as "Cape Dutch" (a term also used to refer collectively to the <a href="/wiki/Cape_Dutch" title="Cape Dutch">early Cape settlers</a>) or "kitchen Dutch" (a derogatory term used to refer to Afrikaans in its earlier days). However, it has also been variously described as a <a href="/wiki/Dutch-based_creole_languages" title="Dutch-based creole languages">Dutch-based</a> <a href="/wiki/Creole_language" title="Creole language">creole</a> or as a partially <a href="/wiki/Creolisation" class="mw-redirect" title="Creolisation">creolised</a> language.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Afrikaans&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Origin">Origin</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Afrikaans&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Origin">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Afrikaans language arose in the <a href="/wiki/Dutch_Cape_Colony" title="Dutch Cape Colony">Dutch Cape Colony</a>, through a gradual divergence from European Dutch dialects, during the course of the 18th century.<sup id="cite_ref-omniglot.com_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-omniglot.com-20">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> As early as the mid-18th century and as recently as the mid-20th century, Afrikaans was known in standard Dutch as a "kitchen language" (Afrikaans: <i lang="af">kombuistaal</i>), lacking the prestige accorded, for example, even by the educational system in Africa, to languages spoken outside Africa. Other early epithets setting apart <i><i lang="nl" title="Dutch language text">Kaaps Hollands</i></i> ("Cape Dutch", i.e. Afrikaans) as putatively beneath official Dutch standards included <i><i lang="nl" title="Dutch language text">geradbraakt</i></i>, <i><i lang="nl" title="Dutch language text">gebroken</i></i> and <i><i lang="nl" title="Dutch language text">onbeschaafd Hollands</i></i> ("mutilated/broken/uncivilised Dutch"), as well as <i><i lang="nl" title="Dutch language text">verkeerd Nederlands</i></i> ("incorrect Dutch").<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<table class="infobox" style="width:22em"><tbody><tr><th colspan="2" class="above" style="text-align:center;font-size:125%;font-weight:bold;font-size:130%; color: black; background-color: tan;">'<span id="Hottentot_Dutch">Hottentot Dutch</span>'</th></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="padding-top:0.225em;line-height:1.1em; padding-right:0.5em;"><div style="padding:0.1em 0;line-height:1.2em;"><span class="wrap"><a href="/wiki/Language_family" title="Language family">Language family</a></span></div></th><td style="line-height:1.35em;"><div style="text-align:left;">Dutch-based <a href="/wiki/Pidgin" title="Pidgin">pidgin</a></div></td></tr><tr><th colspan="2" style="text-align:center;color: black; background-color: tan;">Language codes</th></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="padding-top:0.225em;line-height:1.1em; padding-right:0.5em;"><span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/ISO_639-3" title="ISO 639-3">ISO 639-3</a></span></th><td style="line-height:1.35em;"><i>None</i> (<code>mis</code>)</td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="padding-top:0.225em;line-height:1.1em; padding-right:0.5em;"><a href="/wiki/Glottolog" title="Glottolog">Glottolog</a></th><td style="line-height:1.35em;"><code><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://glottolog.org/resource/languoid/id/hott1234">hott1234</a></code><sup id="cite_ref-Glottolog2_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Glottolog2-24">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Den Besten theorizes that modern Standard Afrikaans derives from two sources:<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> 
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Cape_Dutch" title="Cape Dutch">Cape Dutch</a>, a direct transplantation of European Dutch to Southern Africa, and</li>
<li>'Hottentot Dutch',<sup id="cite_ref-Glottolog2_24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Glottolog2-24">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> a <a href="/wiki/Pidgin" title="Pidgin">pidgin</a> that descended from 'Foreigner Talk' and ultimately from the Dutch pidgin spoken by slaves, via a hypothetical <a href="/wiki/Dutch_creole" class="mw-redirect" title="Dutch creole">Dutch creole</a>.</li></ul>
<p>Thus in his view Afrikaans is neither a creole nor a direct descendant of Dutch, but a fusion of two transmission pathways.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Development">Development</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Afrikaans&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Development">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A relative majority of the first settlers whose descendants today are the <a href="/wiki/Afrikaner" class="mw-redirect" title="Afrikaner">Afrikaners</a> were from the <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_the_Seven_United_Provinces_of_the_Netherlands" class="mw-redirect" title="Republic of the Seven United Provinces of the Netherlands">United Provinces</a> (now <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a> and <a href="/wiki/Flanders" title="Flanders">Flanders</a>),<sup id="cite_ref-zastudy_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-zastudy-26">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> though up to one-sixth of the community was also of French <a href="/wiki/Huguenot" class="mw-redirect" title="Huguenot">Huguenot</a> origin, and a seventh from Germany.<sup id="cite_ref-Britannica1933_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica1933-27">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>African and Asian workers and slaves contributed to the development of Afrikaans. The slave population was made up of people from East Africa, West Africa, <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, <a href="/wiki/Madagascar" title="Madagascar">Madagascar</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Dutch_East_Indies" title="Dutch East Indies">Dutch East Indies</a> (modern <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-Worden_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Worden-28">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> A number were also indigenous <a href="/wiki/Khoisan" title="Khoisan">Khoisan</a> people, who were valued as interpreters, domestic servants, and labourers. Many free and enslaved women married, cohabited with, or were victims of sexual violence from the male Dutch settlers. M. F. Valkhoff argued that 75% of children born to female slaves in the Dutch Cape Colony between 1652 and 1672 had a Dutch father.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThomasonKaufman1988252–254_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThomasonKaufman1988252–254-29">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> Some consider this the origin of the ethnic group, the <a href="/wiki/Cape_Coloured" class="mw-redirect" title="Cape Coloured">Cape Coloureds</a>, who adopted various forms of speech utilising a Dutch vocabulary. Sarah Grey Thomason and Terrence Kaufman argue that Afrikaans' development as a separate language was "heavily conditioned by nonwhites who learned Dutch imperfectly as a second language."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThomasonKaufman1988256_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThomasonKaufman1988256-30">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Beginning in about 1815, Afrikaans started to replace <a href="/wiki/Malay_language" title="Malay language">Malay</a> as the language of instruction in <a href="/wiki/Muslim" class="mw-redirect" title="Muslim">Muslim</a> schools in <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South Africa</a>, written with the <a href="/wiki/Arabic_alphabet" title="Arabic alphabet">Arabic alphabet</a>: see <a href="/wiki/Arabic_Afrikaans" title="Arabic Afrikaans">Arabic Afrikaans</a>. Later, Afrikaans, now written with the <a href="/wiki/Latin_script" title="Latin script">Latin script</a>, started to appear in newspapers and political and religious works in around 1850.<sup id="cite_ref-omniglot.com_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-omniglot.com-20">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1875, a group of Afrikaans-speakers from the Cape formed the <i><i lang="af" title="Afrikaans language text"><a href="/wiki/Genootskap_van_Regte_Afrikaners" title="Genootskap van Regte Afrikaners">Genootskap vir Regte Afrikaanders</a></i></i> ("Society for Real Afrikaners"),<sup id="cite_ref-omniglot.com_20-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-omniglot.com-20">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> and published a number of books in Afrikaans including grammars, dictionaries, religious materials and histories. In 1925, Afrikaans was recognised by the South African government as a real language, rather than simply a slang version of Dutch proper.<sup id="cite_ref-omniglot.com_20-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-omniglot.com-20">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Recognition">Recognition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Afrikaans&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Recognition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Afrikaans_Language_Monument_23.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/The_Afrikaans_Language_Monument_23.JPG/260px-The_Afrikaans_Language_Monument_23.JPG" decoding="async" width="260" height="133" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/The_Afrikaans_Language_Monument_23.JPG/390px-The_Afrikaans_Language_Monument_23.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/The_Afrikaans_Language_Monument_23.JPG/520px-The_Afrikaans_Language_Monument_23.JPG 2x" data-file-width="4320" data-file-height="2202" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Afrikaans_Language_Monument_23.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i lang="af" title="Afrikaans language text">"Dit is ons erns"</i> ("this is our passion"), <span class="nowrap">at the <a href="/wiki/Afrikaans_Language_Monument" title="Afrikaans Language Monument">Afrikaans Language Monument</a></span></div></div></div>
<p>Afrikaans was considered a <a href="/wiki/Dutch_dialect" class="mw-redirect" title="Dutch dialect">Dutch dialect</a> in South Africa until the early 20th century, when it became recognised as a distinct language under South African law, alongside <a href="/wiki/Standard_Dutch" class="mw-redirect" title="Standard Dutch">Standard Dutch</a>, which it eventually replaced as an official language.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> Before the <a href="/wiki/Boer_wars" class="mw-redirect" title="Boer wars">Boer wars</a>, "and indeed for some time afterwards, Afrikaans was regarded as inappropriate for educated discourse. Rather, Afrikaans was described derogatorily as "a kitchen language" or "a bastard jargon," suitable for communication mainly between the Boers and their servants."<sup id="cite_ref-:0_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-32">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint noexcerpt Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:NOTRS" class="mw-redirect" title="Wikipedia:NOTRS"><span title="This claim needs references to better sources. (July 2019)">better&#160;source&#160;needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>On 8 May 1925, twenty-three years after the <a href="/wiki/Second_Boer_War" title="Second Boer War">Second Boer War</a> ended,<sup id="cite_ref-:0_32-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-32">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Official_Languages_of_the_Union_Act,_1925" title="Official Languages of the Union Act, 1925">Official Languages of the Union Act</a> of 1925 was passed—mostly due to the efforts of the <a href="/wiki/Afrikaans_language_movement" title="Afrikaans language movement">Afrikaans language movement</a>—at a joint sitting of the House of Assembly and the Senate, in which the Afrikaans language was declared a variety of Dutch.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/South_African_Constitution_of_1961" title="South African Constitution of 1961">Constitution of 1961</a> reversed the position of Afrikaans and Dutch, so that English and Afrikaans were the official languages, and Afrikaans was deemed to include Dutch. The <a href="/wiki/South_African_Constitution_of_1983" title="South African Constitution of 1983">Constitution of 1983</a> removed any mention of Dutch altogether.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Afrikaans_Language_Monument" title="Afrikaans Language Monument">Afrikaans Language Monument</a> is located on a hill overlooking <a href="/wiki/Paarl" title="Paarl">Paarl</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Western_Cape_Province" class="mw-redirect" title="Western Cape Province">Western Cape Province</a>. Officially opened on 10 October 1975,<sup id="cite_ref-Botha-speech_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Botha-speech-34">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> it was erected on the 100th anniversary of the founding of the <a href="/wiki/Society_of_Real_Afrikaners" class="mw-redirect" title="Society of Real Afrikaners">Society of Real Afrikaners</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> and the 50th anniversary of Afrikaans being declared an official language of South Africa in distinction to Dutch.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Standardisation">Standardisation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Afrikaans&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Standardisation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Pretoriase_kunsmuseum_1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Pretoriase_kunsmuseum_1.jpg/220px-Pretoriase_kunsmuseum_1.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="153" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Pretoriase_kunsmuseum_1.jpg/330px-Pretoriase_kunsmuseum_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Pretoriase_kunsmuseum_1.jpg/440px-Pretoriase_kunsmuseum_1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="558" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Pretoriase_kunsmuseum_1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The side view of the <a href="/wiki/Pretoria_Art_Museum" title="Pretoria Art Museum">Pretoria Art Museum</a> in <a href="/wiki/Arcadia,_Pretoria" title="Arcadia, Pretoria">Arcadia, Pretoria</a>, with an Afrikaans language sign.</div></div></div>
<p>The linguist Paul Roberge suggested the earliest 'truly Afrikaans' texts are <a href="/wiki/Doggerel" title="Doggerel">doggerel verse</a> from 1795 and a dialogue transcribed by a Dutch traveller in 1825. Printed material among the Afrikaners at first used only standard European Dutch. By the mid-19th century, more and more were appearing in Afrikaans, which was very much still regarded as a set of regional dialects.
</p><p>In 1861, L.H. Meurant published his <i><i lang="af" title="Afrikaans language text">Zamenspraak tusschen Klaas Waarzegger en Jan Twyfelaar</i></i> ("Conversation between Claus Truthsayer and John Doubter"), which is considered by some to be the first authoritative Afrikaans text.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> <a href="/wiki/Abu_Bakr_Effendi" title="Abu Bakr Effendi">Abu Bakr Effendi</a> also compiled his <a href="/wiki/Arabic_Afrikaans" title="Arabic Afrikaans">Arabic Afrikaans</a> <a href="/wiki/Islamic" class="mw-redirect" title="Islamic">Islamic</a> instruction book between 1862 and 1869, although this was only published and printed in 1877. The first Afrikaans grammars and dictionaries were published in 1875 by the <i><i lang="af" title="Afrikaans language text"><a href="/wiki/Genootskap_vir_Regte_Afrikaners" class="mw-redirect" title="Genootskap vir Regte Afrikaners">Genootskap vir Regte Afrikaners</a></i></i> ("Society for Real Afrikaners") in <a href="/wiki/Cape_Town" title="Cape Town">Cape Town</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>The main Afrikaans dictionary is the <i><i lang="af" title="Afrikaans language text"><a href="/wiki/Woordeboek_van_die_Afrikaanse_Taal" title="Woordeboek van die Afrikaanse Taal">Woordeboek van die Afrikaanse Taal</a></i></i> (WAT) (<i>Dictionary of the Afrikaans Language</i>), which is as yet incomplete owing to the scale of the project, but the one-volume dictionary in household use is the <i><i lang="af" title="Afrikaans language text"><a href="/wiki/Verklarende_Handwoordeboek_van_die_Afrikaanse_Taal" class="mw-redirect" title="Verklarende Handwoordeboek van die Afrikaanse Taal">Verklarende Handwoordeboek van die Afrikaanse Taal</a></i></i> (HAT). The official <a href="/wiki/Orthography" title="Orthography">orthography</a> of Afrikaans is the <i><i lang="af" title="Afrikaans language text">Afrikaanse Woordelys en Spelreëls</i></i>, compiled by <i lang="af" title="Afrikaans language text"><a href="/wiki/Die_Taalkommissie" title="Die Taalkommissie">Die Taalkommissie</a></i>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Afrikaans_Bible">The Afrikaans Bible</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Afrikaans&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: The Afrikaans Bible">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Bible_translations_into_Afrikaans" title="Bible translations into Afrikaans">Bible translations into Afrikaans</a></div>
<p>The Afrikaner religion had stemmed from the Protestant practices of the Reformed church of Holland during the 17th century, later on being influenced in South Africa by British ministries during the 1800s.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> A landmark in the development of the language was the translation of the whole <a href="/wiki/Bible" title="Bible">Bible</a> into Afrikaans. While significant advances had been made in the textual criticism of the Bible, especially the Greek New Testament, the 1933 translation followed the textus receptus and was closely akin to the <i lang="nl" title="Dutch language text"><a href="/wiki/Statenbijbel" class="mw-redirect" title="Statenbijbel">Statenbijbel</a></i>. Before this, most Cape Dutch-Afrikaans speakers had to rely on the Dutch <i lang="nl" title="Dutch language text"><a href="/wiki/Statenbijbel" class="mw-redirect" title="Statenbijbel">Statenbijbel</a></i>. This <i lang="nl" title="Dutch language text"><a href="/wiki/Statenvertaling" title="Statenvertaling">Statenvertaling</a></i> had its origins with the <a href="/wiki/Synod_of_Dordrecht" class="mw-redirect" title="Synod of Dordrecht">Synod of Dordrecht</a> of 1618 and was thus in an <a href="/wiki/Archaism" title="Archaism">archaic</a> form of Dutch. This was hard for Dutch and Cape Dutch speakers to understand, and increasingly unintelligible for Afrikaans speakers.
</p><p>C. P. Hoogehout, <a href="https://af.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arnoldus_Pannevis" class="extiw" title="af:Arnoldus Pannevis">Arnoldus Pannevis</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Stephanus_Jacobus_du_Toit" title="Stephanus Jacobus du Toit">Stephanus Jacobus du Toit</a> were the first <a href="/wiki/Bible_translations_(Afrikaans)" class="mw-redirect" title="Bible translations (Afrikaans)">Afrikaans Bible</a> translators. Important landmarks in the translation of the Scriptures were in 1878 with C. P. Hoogehout's translation of the <i><i lang="af" title="Afrikaans language text">Evangelie volgens Markus</i></i> (<a href="/wiki/Gospel_of_Mark" title="Gospel of Mark">Gospel of Mark</a>, lit. Gospel according to Mark); however, this translation was never published. The manuscript is to be found in the South African National Library, Cape Town.
</p><p>The first official translation of the entire Bible into Afrikaans was in 1933 by <a href="/wiki/Totius_(poet)" title="Totius (poet)">J. D. du Toit</a>, E. E. van Rooyen, J. D. Kestell, H. C. M. Fourie, and <a href="/wiki/BB_Keet" title="BB Keet">BB Keet</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> This monumental work established Afrikaans as <i><i lang="af" title="Afrikaans language text">'n suiwer en ordentlike taal</i></i>, that is "a pure and proper language" for religious purposes, especially amongst the deeply <a href="/wiki/Calvinist" class="mw-redirect" title="Calvinist">Calvinist</a> Afrikaans religious community that previously had been sceptical of a <a href="/wiki/Bible_translation" class="mw-redirect" title="Bible translation">Bible translation</a> that varied from the Dutch version that they were used to.
</p><p>In 1983, a fresh translation marked the 50th anniversary of the 1933 version and provided a much-needed revision. The final editing of this edition was done by E. P. Groenewald, A. H. van Zyl, P. A. Verhoef, J. L. Helberg and W. Kempen. This translation was influenced by Eugene Nida's theory of dynamic-equavalence which focussed on finding the nearest equavalent in the receptor language to the idea that the Greek, Hebrew or Aramaic wanted to convey. The challenge to this type of translation is that it doesn't take into account that there are shifts in meaning in the receptor language.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>A new translation, <i>Die Bybel: 'n Direkte Vertaling</i> is currently under preparation. It will be the first truly ecumenical translation of the Bible in Afrikaans as translators from various churches, including the Roman Catholic and Anglican Churches, are involved.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>Various commercial translations of the Bible in Afrikaans have also appeared since the 1990s, such as <i>Die Boodskap</i> and the <i>Nuwe Lewende Vertaling</i>. Most of these translations were published by Christelike Uitgewersmaatskappy (CUM).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Vagueness" title="Wikipedia:Vagueness"><span title="This information is too vague. (June 2018)">vague</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Classification">Classification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Afrikaans&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Classification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a href="/wiki/File:West_Germanic_languages_(simplified).png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/1/18/West_Germanic_languages_%28simplified%29.png/400px-West_Germanic_languages_%28simplified%29.png" decoding="async" width="400" height="209" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/1/18/West_Germanic_languages_%28simplified%29.png 1.5x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="268" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:West_Germanic_languages_(simplified).png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The simplified relation between the <a href="/wiki/West_Germanic_languages" title="West Germanic languages">West Germanic languages</a>.</div></div></div>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Indo-European_languages" title="Indo-European languages">Indo-European languages</a>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Germanic_languages" title="Germanic languages">Germanic</a>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/West_Germanic_languages" title="West Germanic languages">West Germanic</a>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Low_Franconian_languages" title="Low Franconian languages">Low Franconian</a>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Dutch_language" title="Dutch language">Dutch</a>
<ul><li>Afrikaans, <a href="/wiki/Dutch-based_creole_languages" title="Dutch-based creole languages">Dutch-based creoles</a></li></ul></li></ul></li></ul></li></ul></li></ul></li></ul>
<p>Afrikaans belongs to its own <a href="/wiki/West_Germanic_languages" title="West Germanic languages">West Germanic</a> sub-group, the <a href="/wiki/Low_Franconian_languages" title="Low Franconian languages">Low Franconian languages</a>. Its closest relative is the <a href="/wiki/Mutual_intelligibility" title="Mutual intelligibility">mutually-intelligible</a> mother language, <a href="/wiki/Dutch_language" title="Dutch language">Dutch</a>. Other West Germanic languages related to Afrikaans are <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a>, <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">English</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Frisian_languages" title="Frisian languages">Frisian languages</a>, and the unstandardised languages <a href="/wiki/Low_German" title="Low German">Low German</a> and <a href="/wiki/Yiddish" title="Yiddish">Yiddish</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geographic_distribution">Geographic distribution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Afrikaans&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Geographic distribution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Statistics">Statistics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Afrikaans&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Statistics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a href="/wiki/File:South_Africa_2011_Afrikaans_speakers_proportion_map.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/South_Africa_2011_Afrikaans_speakers_proportion_map.svg/260px-South_Africa_2011_Afrikaans_speakers_proportion_map.svg.png" decoding="async" width="260" height="228" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/South_Africa_2011_Afrikaans_speakers_proportion_map.svg/390px-South_Africa_2011_Afrikaans_speakers_proportion_map.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/South_Africa_2011_Afrikaans_speakers_proportion_map.svg/520px-South_Africa_2011_Afrikaans_speakers_proportion_map.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="809" data-file-height="709" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:South_Africa_2011_Afrikaans_speakers_proportion_map.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The geographical distribution of Afrikaans in South Africa: proportion of the population that speaks Afrikaans at home. <div class="div-col columns column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sociolinguistics">Sociolinguistics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Afrikaans&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Sociolinguistics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Some state that instead of <i>Afrikaners</i>, which refers to an ethnic group, the terms <i><i lang="af" title="Afrikaans language text">Afrikaanses</i></i> or <i><i lang="af" title="Afrikaans language text">Afrikaanssprekendes</i></i> (lit. Afrikaans speakers) should be used for people of any ethnic origin who speak Afrikaans. Linguistic identity has not yet established which terms shall prevail, and all three are used in common parlance.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> Afrikaans terms like <i><i lang="af" title="Afrikaans language text">boerseun</i></i> (farm boy) and <i><i lang="af" title="Afrikaans language text">boeremeisie</i></i> (farm girl) became popular among young white Afrikaners for expressing ethnic and cultural pride, regardless of whether or not they actually grew up on a farm.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a href="/wiki/File:South_Africa_2011_Afrikaans_speakers_density_map.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/South_Africa_2011_Afrikaans_speakers_density_map.svg/260px-South_Africa_2011_Afrikaans_speakers_density_map.svg.png" decoding="async" width="260" height="228" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/South_Africa_2011_Afrikaans_speakers_density_map.svg/390px-South_Africa_2011_Afrikaans_speakers_density_map.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/South_Africa_2011_Afrikaans_speakers_density_map.svg/520px-South_Africa_2011_Afrikaans_speakers_density_map.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="809" data-file-height="709" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:South_Africa_2011_Afrikaans_speakers_density_map.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The geographical distribution of Afrikaans in South Africa: density of Afrikaans home-language speakers. <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886047268">.mw-parser-output .refbegin{font-size:90%;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .refbegin-hanging-indents>ul{list-style-type:none;margin-left:0}.mw-parser-output .refbegin-hanging-indents>ul>li,.mw-parser-output .refbegin-hanging-indents>dl>dd{margin-left:0;padding-left:3.2em;text-indent:-3.2em;list-style:none}.mw-parser-output .refbegin-100{font-size:100%}</style><div class="refbegin columns references-column-count references-column-count-2" style="-moz-column-count: 2; -webkit-column-count: 2; column-count: 2;"> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #ffffcc; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;&lt;1&#160;/km<sup>2</sup></div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #ffeda0; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;1–3&#160;/km<sup>2</sup></div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #fed976; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;3–10&#160;/km<sup>2</sup></div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #feb24c; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;10–30&#160;/km<sup>2</sup></div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #fd8d3c; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;30–100&#160;/km<sup>2</sup></div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #fc4e2a; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;100–300&#160;/km<sup>2</sup></div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #e31a1c; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;300–1000&#160;/km<sup>2</sup></div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #bc0026; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;1000–3000&#160;/km<sup>2</sup></div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #800026; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;&gt;3000&#160;/km<sup>2</sup></div> </div></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Distribution_of_Afrikaans_in_Namibia.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Distribution_of_Afrikaans_in_Namibia.png/220px-Distribution_of_Afrikaans_in_Namibia.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="203" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Distribution_of_Afrikaans_in_Namibia.png/330px-Distribution_of_Afrikaans_in_Namibia.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Distribution_of_Afrikaans_in_Namibia.png/440px-Distribution_of_Afrikaans_in_Namibia.png 2x" data-file-width="975" data-file-height="900" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Distribution_of_Afrikaans_in_Namibia.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The geographical distribution of Afrikaans in <a href="/wiki/Namibia" title="Namibia">Namibia</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Afrikaans is also widely spoken in Namibia. Before independence, Afrikaans had equal status with German as an official language. Since independence in 1990, Afrikaans has had constitutional recognition as a national, but not official, language.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> There is a much smaller number of Afrikaans speakers among Zimbabwe's white minority, as most have left the country since 1980. Afrikaans was also a medium of instruction for schools in <a href="/wiki/Bophuthatswana" title="Bophuthatswana">Bophuthatswana</a>, an Apartheid-era <a href="/wiki/Bantustan" title="Bantustan">Bantustan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Eldoret" title="Eldoret">Eldoret</a> in Kenya was founded by Afrikaners.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Many South Africans living and working in Belgium, the Netherlands, the United Kingdom, Republic of Ireland, Australia, New Zealand, Canada, the United States, the <a href="/wiki/UAE" class="mw-redirect" title="UAE">UAE</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kuwait" title="Kuwait">Kuwait</a> are also Afrikaans-speaking. They have access to Afrikaans websites, news sites such as <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.netwerk24.com/">Netwerk24.com</a> and <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.sake24.com/">Sake24</a>, and radio broadcasts over the web, such as those from <a href="/wiki/Radio_Sonder_Grense" title="Radio Sonder Grense">Radio Sonder Grense</a>, <a href="/w/index.php?title=Bokradio&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Bokradio (page does not exist)">Bokradio</a> and <a href="/wiki/Radio_Pretoria" title="Radio Pretoria">Radio Pretoria</a>.
</p><p>Afrikaans has been influential in the development of <a href="/wiki/South_African_English" title="South African English">South African English</a>. Many Afrikaans loanwords have found their way into South African English, such as <i><i lang="af" title="Afrikaans language text"><a href="/wiki/Bakkie" class="mw-redirect" title="Bakkie">bakkie</a></i></i> ("pickup truck"), <i><i lang="af" title="Afrikaans language text"><a href="/wiki/Braai" class="mw-redirect" title="Braai">braai</a></i></i> ("barbecue"), <i><i lang="af" title="Afrikaans language text"><a href="/wiki/Naartjie" class="mw-redirect" title="Naartjie">naartjie</a></i></i> ("tangerine"), <i><i lang="af" title="Afrikaans language text">tekkies</i></i> (American "sneakers", British "trainers", Canadian "runners"). A few words in standard English are derived from Afrikaans, such as <i><a href="/wiki/Aardvark" title="Aardvark">aardvark</a></i> (lit. "earth pig"), <i><a href="/wiki/Trekking" class="mw-redirect" title="Trekking">trek</a></i> ("pioneering journey", in Afrikaans lit. "pull" but used also for "migrate"), <i>spoor</i> ("animal track"), <i><i lang="af" title="Afrikaans language text">veld</i></i> ("Southern African grassland" in Afrikaans, lit. "field"), <i>commando</i> from Afrikaans <i><i lang="af" title="Afrikaans language text">kommando</i></i> meaning small fighting unit, <i><i lang="af" title="Afrikaans language text">boomslang</i></i> ("tree snake") and <i><i lang="af" title="Afrikaans language text"><a href="/wiki/Apartheid" title="Apartheid">apartheid</a></i></i> ("segregation"; more accurately "apartness" or "the state or condition of being apart").
</p><p>In 1976, secondary-school pupils in <a href="/wiki/Soweto" title="Soweto">Soweto</a> began <a href="/wiki/Soweto_riots" class="mw-redirect" title="Soweto riots">a rebellion</a> in response to the government's decision that Afrikaans be used as the language of instruction for half the subjects taught in non-White schools (with English continuing for the other half). Although English is the mother tongue of only 8.2% of the population, it is the language most widely understood, and the second language of a majority of South Africans.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> Afrikaans is more widely spoken than English in the Northern and Western Cape provinces, several hundred kilometres from Soweto.<sup id="cite_ref-auto_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-49">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Black community's opposition to Afrikaans and preference for continuing English instruction was underlined when the government rescinded the policy one month after the uprising: 96% of Black schools chose English (over Afrikaans or native languages) as the language of instruction.<sup id="cite_ref-auto_49-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-49">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> Also, due to Afrikaans being viewed as the "language of the white oppressor" by some, pressure has been increased to remove Afrikaans as a teaching language in South African universities, resulting in bloody student protests in 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Under <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_South_Africa" title="Constitution of South Africa">South Africa's Constitution</a> of 1996, Afrikaans remains an official language, and has equal status to English and nine other languages. The new policy means that the use of Afrikaans is now often reduced in favour of English, or to accommodate the other official languages. In 1996, for example, the <a href="/wiki/South_African_Broadcasting_Corporation" title="South African Broadcasting Corporation">South African Broadcasting Corporation</a> reduced the amount of television airtime in Afrikaans, while <a href="/wiki/South_African_Airways" title="South African Airways">South African Airways</a> dropped its Afrikaans name <i><i lang="af" title="Afrikaans language text">Suid-Afrikaanse Lugdiens</i></i> from its <a href="/wiki/Livery" title="Livery">livery</a>. Similarly, South Africa's <a href="/wiki/Diplomatic_mission" title="Diplomatic mission">diplomatic missions</a> overseas now only display the name of the country in English and their host country's language, and not in Afrikaans.
</p><p>In spite of these moves, the language has remained strong, and Afrikaans newspapers and magazines continue to have large circulation figures. Indeed, the Afrikaans-language general-interest family magazine <i><i lang="af" title="Afrikaans language text"><a href="/wiki/Huisgenoot" title="Huisgenoot">Huisgenoot</a></i></i> has the largest readership of any magazine in the country.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> In addition, a pay-TV channel in Afrikaans called <a href="/wiki/KykNet" class="mw-redirect" title="KykNet">KykNet</a> was launched in 1999, and an Afrikaans music channel, <a href="/wiki/MK_(channel)" title="MK (channel)">MK</a> (<i><i lang="af" title="Afrikaans language text">Musiek kanaal</i></i>) (lit. 'Music Channel'), in 2005. A large number of Afrikaans books are still published every year, mainly by the publishers Human &amp; Rousseau, <i lang="af" title="Afrikaans language text">Tafelberg Uitgewers</i>, <i lang="af" title="Afrikaans language text">Struik</i>, and <i lang="af" title="Afrikaans language text">Protea Boekhuis</i>. The Afrikaans film trilogy <i><i lang="af" title="Afrikaans language text"><a href="/wiki/Bakgat" title="Bakgat">Bakgat</a></i></i> (first released in 2008) caused a reawakening of the Afrikaans film Industry (which has been dead since the mid to late 1990s) and Belgian-born singer <a href="/wiki/Karen_Zoid" title="Karen Zoid">Karen Zoid</a>'s debut single "<i lang="af" title="Afrikaans language text"><a href="/wiki/Afrikaners_is_Plesierig" class="mw-redirect" title="Afrikaners is Plesierig">Afrikaners is Plesierig</a></i>" (released 2001) caused a resurgence in the Afrikaans music industry as well as gave rise to the Afrikaans Rock genre.
</p><p>Afrikaans has two monuments erected in its honour. The first was erected in <i lang="af" title="Afrikaans language text"><a href="/wiki/Burgersdorp" title="Burgersdorp">Burgersdorp</a></i>, South Africa, in 1893, and the second, nowadays better-known <a href="/wiki/Afrikaans_Language_Monument" title="Afrikaans Language Monument">Afrikaans Language Monument</a> (<i><i lang="af" title="Afrikaans language text">Afrikaanse Taalmonument</i></i>), was built in <a href="/wiki/Paarl" title="Paarl">Paarl</a>, South Africa, in 1975.
</p><p>When the British design magazine <i><a href="/wiki/Wallpaper_(magazine)" title="Wallpaper (magazine)">Wallpaper</a></i> described Afrikaans as "one of the world's ugliest languages" in its September 2005 article about the <a href="/wiki/Afrikaans_Language_Monument" title="Afrikaans Language Monument">monument</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> South African <a href="/wiki/Billionaire" title="Billionaire">billionaire</a> <a href="/wiki/Johann_Rupert" title="Johann Rupert">Johann Rupert</a> (chairman of the <a href="/wiki/Richemont" title="Richemont">Richemont Group</a>), responded by withdrawing advertising for brands such as <a href="/wiki/Cartier_SA" class="mw-redirect" title="Cartier SA">Cartier</a>, <a href="/wiki/Van_Cleef_%26_Arpels" title="Van Cleef &amp; Arpels">Van Cleef &amp; Arpels</a>, <a href="/wiki/Montblanc_(pens)" class="mw-redirect" title="Montblanc (pens)">Montblanc</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Dunhill" title="Alfred Dunhill">Alfred Dunhill</a> from the magazine.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> The author of the article, Bronwyn Davies, was an <a href="/wiki/South_African_English" title="South African English">English</a>-speaking South African.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Mutual_intelligibility_with_Dutch">Mutual intelligibility with Dutch</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Afrikaans&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Mutual intelligibility with Dutch">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>An estimated 90 to 95% of the Afrikaans lexicon is ultimately of Dutch origin,<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> and there are few lexical differences between the two languages.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> Afrikaans has a considerably more regular morphology,<sup id="cite_ref-holm_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-holm-60">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> grammar, and spelling.<sup id="cite_ref-sebba_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sebba-61">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> There is a degree of <a href="/wiki/Mutual_intelligibility" title="Mutual intelligibility">mutual intelligibility</a> between the two languages,<sup id="cite_ref-holm_60-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-holm-60">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-baker_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-baker-62">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> particularly in written form.<sup id="cite_ref-sebba_61-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sebba-61">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-sebba2_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sebba2-64">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-gooskens_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gooskens-65">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Afrikaans acquired some lexical and syntactical borrowings from other languages such as <a href="/wiki/Malay_language" title="Malay language">Malay</a>, <a href="/wiki/Khoisan_languages" title="Khoisan languages">Khoisan languages</a>, <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_language" title="Portuguese language">Portuguese</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-deumert_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-deumert-66">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Bantu_languages" title="Bantu languages">Bantu languages</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-niesler_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-niesler-67">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> and Afrikaans has also been significantly influenced by <a href="/wiki/South_African_English" title="South African English">South African English</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> Dutch speakers are confronted with fewer non-cognates when listening to Afrikaans than the other way round.<sup id="cite_ref-gooskens_65-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gooskens-65">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> Mutual intelligibility thus tends to be asymmetrical, as it is easier for Dutch speakers to understand Afrikaans than for Afrikaans speakers to understand Dutch.<sup id="cite_ref-gooskens_65-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gooskens-65">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In general, mutual intelligibility between Dutch and Afrikaans is better than between Dutch and <a href="/wiki/West_Frisian_language" title="West Frisian language">Frisian</a><sup id="cite_ref-thije_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-thije-69">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> or <a href="/wiki/North_Germanic_languages#Mutual_intelligibility" title="North Germanic languages">between</a> <a href="/wiki/Danish_language" title="Danish language">Danish</a> and <a href="/wiki/Swedish_language" title="Swedish language">Swedish</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-gooskens_65-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gooskens-65">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> The South African poet writer <a href="/wiki/Breyten_Breytenbach" title="Breyten Breytenbach">Breyten Breytenbach</a>, attempting to visualise the language distance for <a href="/wiki/English-speaking_world" title="English-speaking world">anglophones</a> once remarked that the differences between (Standard) Dutch and Afrikaans are comparable to those between the <a href="/wiki/Received_Pronunciation" title="Received Pronunciation">Received Pronunciation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Southern_American_English" title="Southern American English">Southern American English</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Current_status">Current status</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Afrikaans&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Current status">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

</td></tr>
<tr>

</th></tr></tbody></table>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Post-apartheid_South_Africa" class="mw-redirect" title="Post-apartheid South Africa">Post-apartheid South Africa</a> has seen a loss of preferential treatment by the government for Afrikaans, in terms of education, social events, media (TV and radio), and general status throughout the country, given that it now shares its place as official language with ten other languages. Nevertheless, Afrikaans remains more prevalent in the media – radio, newspapers and television<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> – than any of the other official languages, except English. More than 300 book titles in Afrikaans are published annually.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> South African census figures suggest a growing number of speakers in all nine provinces, a total of 6.85 million in 2011 compared to 5.98 million a decade earlier.<sup id="cite_ref-Beeld-2012_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Beeld-2012-76">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> The South African Institute of Race Relations (SAIRR) project that a growing majority will be <a href="/wiki/Coloured" class="mw-redirect" title="Coloured">Coloured</a> Afrikaans speakers.<sup id="cite_ref-Rapport-2013_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rapport-2013-77">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> Afrikaans speakers experience higher employment rates than other South African language groups, though as of 2012 half a million remain unemployed.<sup id="cite_ref-Beeld-2012_76-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Beeld-2012-76">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Despite the challenges of demotion and emigration that it faces in South Africa, the Afrikaans vernacular remains competitive, being popular in <a href="/wiki/DSTV" class="mw-redirect" title="DSTV">DSTV</a> pay channels and several internet sites, while generating high newspaper and music CD sales. A resurgence in Afrikaans popular music since the late 1990s has invigorated the language, especially among a younger generation of South Africans. A recent trend is the increased availability of pre-school educational CDs and DVDs. Such media also prove popular with the extensive Afrikaans-speaking expatriate communities who seek to retain language proficiency in a household context.
</p><p>After years of slumber, Afrikaans language cinema is showing signs of new vigour. The 2007 film <i><i lang="af" title="Afrikaans language text">Ouma se slim kind</i></i>, the first full-length Afrikaans movie since <i><a href="/wiki/Paljas" title="Paljas">Paljas</a></i> in 1998, is seen as the dawn of a new era in Afrikaans cinema. Several short films have been created and more feature-length movies, such as <i><i lang="af" title="Afrikaans language text">Poena is Koning</i></i> and <i><i lang="af" title="Afrikaans language text"><a href="/wiki/Bakgat" title="Bakgat">Bakgat</a></i></i> (both in 2008) have been produced, besides the 2011 Afrikaans-language film <i><i lang="af" title="Afrikaans language text"><a href="/wiki/Skoonheid" class="mw-redirect" title="Skoonheid">Skoonheid</a></i></i>, which was the first Afrikaans film to screen at the <a href="/wiki/Cannes_Film_Festival" title="Cannes Film Festival">Cannes Film Festival</a>. The film <i><i lang="af" title="Afrikaans language text">Platteland</i></i> was also released in 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> The Afrikaans Film industry started gaining international recognition via the likes of big Afrikaans Hollywood film stars, like <a href="/wiki/Charlize_Theron" title="Charlize Theron">Charlize Theron</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Monster_(2003_film)" title="Monster (2003 film)">Monster</a></i>) and <a href="/wiki/Sharlto_Copley" title="Sharlto Copley">Sharlto Copley</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/District_9" title="District 9">District 9</a></i>) promoting their mother tongue.
</p><p>Afrikaans seems to be returning to the <a href="/wiki/SABC" class="mw-redirect" title="SABC">SABC</a>. SABC3 announced early in 2009 that it would increase Afrikaans programming due to the "growing Afrikaans-language market and [their] need for working capital as Afrikaans advertising is the only advertising that sells in the current South African television market". In April 2009, SABC3 started screening several Afrikaans-language programmes.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> Further latent support for the language derives from its de-politicised image in the eyes of younger-generation South Africans, who less and less often view it as "the language of the oppressor".<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2010)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Indeed, there is a groundswell movement within Afrikaans to be inclusive, and to promote itself along with the other indigenous official languages. In Namibia, the percentage of Afrikaans speakers declined from 11.4% (2001 Census) to 10.4% (2011 Census). The major concentrations are in Hardap (41.0%), ǁKaras (36.1%), Erongo (20.5%), Khomas (18.5%), Omaheke (10.0%), Otjozondjupa (9.4%), Kunene (4.2%), and Oshikoto (2.3%).<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Many native speakers of <a href="/wiki/Bantu_languages" title="Bantu languages">Bantu languages</a> and <a href="/wiki/South_African_English" title="South African English">English</a> also speak Afrikaans as a second language. It is widely taught in South African schools, with about 10.3 million second-language students.<sup id="cite_ref-e19_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-e19-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> Even in KwaZulu-Natal (where there are relatively few Afrikaans home-speakers), the majority of pupils opt for Afrikaans as their first additional language because it is regarded as easier than Zulu.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Afrikaans is offered at many universities outside South Africa, for example in the Netherlands, Belgium, Germany, Poland, Russia and America.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Grammar">Grammar</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Afrikaans&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Grammar">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Afrikaans_grammar" title="Afrikaans grammar">Afrikaans grammar</a></div><table class="box-Unreferenced_section plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Unreferenced" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><a href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" decoding="async" width="50" height="39" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="399" /></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>does not <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">cite</a> any <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">sources</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Afrikaans&amp;action=edit">improve this section</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Burden_of_evidence" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">removed</a>.</span>  <small class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">September 2019</span>)</i></small><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>In Afrikaans grammar, there is no distinction between the <a href="/wiki/Infinitive" title="Infinitive">infinitive</a> and present forms of verbs, with the exception of the verbs 'to be' and 'to have':
</p>
<table class="wikitable">

<tbody><tr style="background:#ffdead;">
<th>infinitive form
</th>
<th>present indicative form
</th>
<th>Dutch
</th>
<th>English
</th>
<th>German
</th></tr>
<tr>
<td><i lang="af" title="Afrikaans language text">wees</i></td>
<td><i lang="af" title="Afrikaans language text">is</i></td>
<td><i lang="nl" title="Dutch language text">zijn</i> (<i lang="nl" title="Dutch language text">wezen</i>)</td>
<td>be</td>
<td><i lang="de" title="German language text">sein</i> (<i lang="de" title="German language text">gewesen</i>)
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><i lang="af" title="Afrikaans language text">hê</i></td>
<td><i lang="af" title="Afrikaans language text">het</i></td>
<td><i lang="nl" title="Dutch language text">hebben</i></td>
<td>have</td>
<td><i lang="de" title="German language text">haben</i>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>In addition, verbs do not <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_conjugation" title="Grammatical conjugation">conjugate</a> differently depending on the subject. For example,
</p>

</td></tr>

<td>you are (sing.)</td>
<td><i lang="de" title="German language text">du bist/Sie sind</i>

</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><i lang="af" title="Afrikaans language text">hulle is</i></td>

<td>they are</td>

<p>Only a handful of Afrikaans verbs have a <a href="/wiki/Preterite" title="Preterite">preterite</a>, namely the auxiliary <i><i lang="af" title="Afrikaans language text">wees</i></i> ("to be"), the <a href="/wiki/Modal_verbs" class="mw-redirect" title="Modal verbs">modal verbs</a>, and the verb <i><i lang="af" title="Afrikaans language text">dink</i></i> ("to think"). The preterite of <i><i lang="af" title="Afrikaans language text">mag</i></i> ("may") is rare in contemporary Afrikaans.
</p>
<table class="wikitable">

<tbody><tr style="background:#ffdead;">

<td>I think</td>

<p>All other verbs use the perfect tense (<i>hê</i> + past participle) for the past. Therefore, there is no distinction in Afrikaans between <i>I drank</i> and <i>I have drunk</i>. (Also in colloquial German, the past tense is often replaced with the perfect.)  
</p>
<table class="wikitable">

<tbody><tr style="background:#ffdead;">
<th>Afrikaans</th>
<th>Dutch</th>
<th>English</th>
<th>German
</th></tr>
<tr>
<td rowspan="2"><i lang="af" title="Afrikaans language text">ek het gedrink</i></td>
<td><i lang="nl" title="Dutch language text">ik dronk</i></td>
<td>I drank</td>
<td><i lang="de" title="German language text">ich trank</i> <small>(formal)</small>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><i lang="nl" title="Dutch language text">ik heb gedronken</i></td>
<td>I have drunk</td>
<td><i lang="de" title="German language text">ich habe getrunken</i>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>When telling a longer story, Afrikaans speakers usually avoid the perfect and simply use the present tense, or <a href="/wiki/Historical_present_tense" class="mw-redirect" title="Historical present tense">historical present tense</a> instead (as is possible, but less common, in English as well).
</p><p>A particular feature of Afrikaans is its use of the <a href="/wiki/Double_negative" title="Double negative">double negative</a>; it is classified in Afrikaans as <i><i lang="af" title="Afrikaans language text">ontkennende vorm</i></i> and is something that is absent from the other West Germanic standard languages. For example,
</p>
<dl><dd>Afrikaans: <i lang="af">Hy kan <b>nie</b> Afrikaans praat <b>nie</b></i>, <small><abbr title="literal translation">lit.</abbr>&#160;</small>&#39;He can not Afrikaans speak not&#39;</dd>
<dd>Dutch: <i lang="nl">Hij spreekt <b>geen</b> Afrikaans.</i> / <i><a href="/wiki/Dutch_language" title="Dutch language">Dutch</a>: <i lang="nl">Hij kan <b>geen</b> Afrikaans praten.</i></i></dd>
<dd>English: He speaks <i><b>no</b></i> Afrikaans. / He can <i><b>not</b></i> speak Afrikaans. / He <i><b>can't</b></i> speak Afrikaans.</dd>
<dd>German: <i lang="de">Er spricht <b>kein</b> Afrikaans.</i></dd></dl>
<p>Both French and San origins have been suggested for double negation in Afrikaans. While double negation is still found in <a href="/w/index.php?title=Low_Franconian_dialects&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Low Franconian dialects (page does not exist)">Low Franconian dialects</a> in <a href="/wiki/West-Flanders" class="mw-redirect" title="West-Flanders">West-Flanders</a> and in some "isolated" villages in the centre of the Netherlands (such as <a href="/wiki/Garderen" title="Garderen">Garderen</a>), it takes a different form, which is not found in Afrikaans. The following is an example:
</p>
<dl><dd>Afrikaans: <i lang="af">Ek wil dit nie doen nie.</i><sup>*</sup> (<i>lit.</i> I want this not do not.)</dd>
<dd>Dutch: <i lang="nl">Ik wil dit niet doen.</i></dd>
<dd>English: I do not want to do this.</dd>
<dd>German: <i lang="de">Ich will dies nicht tun.</i></dd></dl>
<p><sup>*</sup> Compare with <i><i lang="af" title="Afrikaans language text">Ek wil nie dit doen nie</i></i>, which changes the meaning to "I want not to do this." Whereas <i><i lang="af" title="Afrikaans language text">Ek wil nie dit doen nie</i></i> emphasizes a lack of desire to act, <i><i lang="af" title="Afrikaans language text">Ek wil dit nie doen nie</i></i> emphasizes the act itself.
</p><p>The <i><i lang="dum" title="Middle Dutch (ca. 1050-1350) language text">-ne</i></i> was the Middle Dutch way to negate but it has been suggested that since <i><i lang="dum" title="Middle Dutch (ca. 1050-1350) language text">-ne</i></i> became highly non-voiced, <i><b><i lang="dum" title="Middle Dutch (ca. 1050-1350) language text">nie</i></b></i> or <i><b><i lang="dum" title="Middle Dutch (ca. 1050-1350) language text">niet</i></b></i> was needed to complement the <i><i lang="dum" title="Middle Dutch (ca. 1050-1350) language text">-ne</i></i>. With time the <i><i lang="dum" title="Middle Dutch (ca. 1050-1350) language text">-ne</i></i> disappeared in most Dutch dialects.
</p><p>The double negative construction has been fully grammaticalised in standard Afrikaans and its proper use follows a set of fairly complex rules as the examples below show:
</p>
<table class="wikitable">

<tbody><tr>
<th>Afrikaans
</th>
<th>Dutch (literally translated)
</th>
<th>More correct Dutch
</th>
<th>English
</th></tr>
<tr>
<td><i lang="af" title="Afrikaans language text">Ek het nie geweet dat hy sou kom nie.</i>
</td>
<td><i lang="nl" title="Dutch language text">Ik heb niet geweten dat hij zou komen.</i>
</td>
<td><i lang="nl" title="Dutch language text">Ik wist niet dat hij zou komen.</i>
</td>
<td>I did not know that he would come.
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><i lang="af" title="Afrikaans language text">Ek het geweet dat hy nie sou kom nie.</i>
</td>
<td><i lang="nl" title="Dutch language text">Ik heb geweten dat hij niet zou komen.</i>
</td>
<td><i lang="nl" title="Dutch language text">Ik wist dat hij niet zou komen.</i>
</td>
<td>I knew (did know) that he would not come.
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><i lang="af" title="Afrikaans language text">Ek het nie geweet dat hy nie sou kom nie.</i>
</td>
<td><i lang="nl" title="Dutch language text">Ik heb niet geweten dat hij niet zou komen.</i>
</td>
<td><i lang="nl" title="Dutch language text">Ik wist niet dat hij niet zou komen.</i>
</td>
<td>I did not know that he would not come.
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><i lang="af" title="Afrikaans language text">Hy sal<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> nie kom nie, want hy is siek.</i>
</td>
<td><i lang="nl" title="Dutch language text">Hij zal niet komen, want hij is ziek.</i>
</td>
<td><i lang="nl" title="Dutch language text">Hij komt niet, want hij is ziek.</i>
</td>
<td>He will not come, as he is sick.
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><i lang="af" title="Afrikaans language text">Dis (Dit is) nie so moeilik om Afrikaans te leer nie.</i>
</td>
<td colspan="2" align="center"><i lang="nl" title="Dutch language text">Het is niet zo moeilijk (om) Afrikaans te leren.</i>
</td>
<td>It is not so difficult to learn Afrikaans.
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>A notable exception to this is the use of the negating grammar form that coincides with negating the English present participle. In this case there is only a single negation.
</p>
<dl><dd>Afrikaans: <i lang="af">Hy is in die hospitaal, maar hy eet nie.</i></dd>
<dd>Dutch: <i lang="nl">Hij is in het ziekenhuis, maar hij eet niet.</i></dd>
<dd>English: He is in [the] hospital, though he eats not.</dd>
<dd>German: <i lang="de">Er ist im Krankenhaus, aber er isst nicht.</i></dd></dl>
<p>Certain words in Afrikaans arise due to grammar. For example, <i><i lang="af" title="Afrikaans language text">moet nie</i></i>, which literally means "must not", usually becomes <i><i lang="af" title="Afrikaans language text">moenie</i></i>; although one does not have to write or say it like this, virtually all Afrikaans speakers will change the two words to <i><i lang="af" title="Afrikaans language text">moenie</i></i> in the same way as <i>do not</i> shifts to <i>don't</i> in English.
</p><p>The Dutch word <i><i lang="nl" title="Dutch language text">het</i></i> ("it" in English) does not correspond to <i><i lang="af" title="Afrikaans language text">het</i></i> in Afrikaans. The Dutch words corresponding to Afrikaans <i><i lang="af" title="Afrikaans language text">het</i></i> are <i><i lang="nl" title="Dutch language text">heb</i></i>, <i><i lang="nl" title="Dutch language text">hebt</i></i>, <i><i lang="nl" title="Dutch language text">heeft</i></i> and <i><i lang="nl" title="Dutch language text">hebben</i></i>.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Phonology">Phonology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Afrikaans&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Phonology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Afrikaans_phonology" title="Afrikaans phonology">Afrikaans phonology</a></div>
